To Our Advertisers:
We extend our gratitude, not only for making this
publication possible, but also for the support you
lend to our many activities. We urge our members
to reciprocate with their patronage.
About the Cover:
The beautiful portrait, painted in oils by artist
Blanche Slott, lovingly captures Rabbi Dov Kentof
enjoying two of his passions—Judaism and the great
outdoors. Blanche created this new work especially
foi this occasion, as a gift to Rabbi Kentof.
With Deep Appreciation and Affection

is Dedicated to
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Director of Education and Youth
who, for 20 years, has worked tirelessly on behalf
of the youth and families of our congregation, our
community and throughout the Southeast.

tributes to Rabbi Kentof, begins on page 3.

This year we celebrate the end of the century, and The Florida Times-Umon
wants Jacksonville to celebrate more than the changing of the calendar. This
is a great community and we have a lot to celebrate in our past and in the
future. Throughout this year and into the next, the Times-Umon is planning
events, features and special sections that carry the Celebrate 2000 theme. Get
in the spirit. Join us in the celebration.
To be a Celebrate 2000 partner, call (904) 359-4686.

===== PAGE 5 =====
— Mazel Tov & Memories —
a special section to honor
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Director of Education and Youth
for twenty years of devoted service to the families of the Jacksonville Jewish Center
Rabbi Dov Kentof is a modest, gracious man who
is quick to praise the achievements of others. He is a
man more of action than of words, yet his stories are
filled with insight. It is fitting at this milestone in his
life, and ours, that we reflect on
his achievements and give some
insights into the story of his life.
Rabbi Kentof was bom in
Tel Aviv at the time when Israel
was struggling for statehood. At
age 18, Dov served his country
in the Navy. He attended
college at Hebrew University,
in Israel, where he received a
bachelor’s degree in history and
a master’s degree in education.
When he came to the United
States in 1960, Dov enrolled in
rabbinical school, and was
ordained in 1973. His loving
wife Alina is his soulmate,
mentor, colleague and friend.
Their son, Gil, is a licensed
chiropractor and owner of Back
Pain Chiropractic Center in
Franklin, Tennessee. Daughter
Tali Whitely is a 6th grade
teacher, wife and mother of five
year old Zachary, in Tampa,
Florida.
Among his talents, Rabbi
Kentof is the author of Games
for Keeps, published by the Central Agency of Jewish
Education. He is an expert camper, who has created
unique bonding and Jewish-identity experiences for
youth and families in many different outdoor settings.
His whitewater rafting trips on the Nolichucky River in
North Carolina, for groups of 40-50 children and adults,
have become legendary. A wonderful tour guide, Dov
spent six consecutive summers leading USY groups to
Israel, conducted two teen pilgrimages to Israel and
Poland as well as several group tours. He is a harmonica
virtuoso, a great cook and a prolific photographer.
Rabbi Kentof has a passion for working with young
people. It is his greatest love and his greatest gift. It
shows in everything he does, and it is returned by the
youth whose lives are fired-up by his involvement.
Over the past 20 years,
thousands of children of our
congregation, and throughout
the Southeast, have flourished
Jewishly through Rabbi Dov
Kentof’s innovative programs,
unique educational style and
genuine concern for the
individual. He has drawn in the
fringes of our community like
the corners of his own talit,
instilling a sense of belonging. He
has reached out to those in need,
giving hope and encouragement.
He has demonstrated his love of
Torah in all he does.
A natural leader, Rabbi
Kentof has an uncanny ability to
bring people together to plan and
work on projects of epic
proportions that always succeed
beyond expectation.His large
scale presentations of Noah’s Ark,
Fantasy Flights to Israel, The
World of Our Grandparents, to
name a few, have captured the
hearts and imaginations of
children and adults alike, and
have garnered for the Center numerous awards for
excellence. Among them has been the coveted Solomon
Schechter Award for Family Programming given by the
United Synagogue of America.
Perhaps Rabbi Kentof’s greatest achievements are
the many lifelong bonds he has made with children and
adults. The following pages capture priceless moments
and heartfelt expressions of gratitude and affection from
the many people he has touched with his warm smile,
patient understanding and unflagging loyalty.
“A hundred years from now ... it
will not matter what your banH account
was, the sort of house you lived in, or
the Hind of car you drove ... but the
world will be a better place because you
were important in the life of a child."
— Guardian Ad Litem creed,
quoted by Arlene Friedman
3

In Honor Of
Rabbi Dov Kentof
My Father
by Gil Kentof
I have many fond childhood memories of times spent with my father. I
have specific memories of a one week hiking, canoeing and rock climbing trip in
North Carolina and many Passover seders with my family at home. The most vivid
of these memories, however, are the trips my father and I made together to Lake
City, Florida. I was to obtain my falconry license and ultimately catch a wild hawk.
I'm certain this sounds quite strange and unusual but they are memories I will
always cherish.
I was fifteen years old at the time 1 decided to learn every aspect of this
ancient sport. After passing a difficult exam, and several 5tate equipment
Inspections, with lots of encouragement from my father, I became the youngest
falconer in the 5tate of Florida. That winter my father and I proceeded down the
highways in search of a young migrating red tail hawk. On Sundays, (not Shabbat)
we would rise at 5 a.m. in 30 degree temperatures to catch just the right hawk. I
now think to myself, "What other father would do such mishegas?"
During those years, my father taught me some extraordinary lessons
through his actions. I learned how persistence alone is omnipotent and how the
love of nature can bring you closer to God and Judaism. Through those lessons, I
also learned to pursue my dreams, full steam ahead!
I finally caught my hawk and practiced the wonderful sport of falconry, but
my fondest memories are those mornings I spent with my Dad!
for 20 years of devotion to the youth of our congregation

In Honor Of
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Feeling Was Always First
by Tali Kentof Whitely
"5ince feeling Is first, those who pay attention to the syntax of things will never
wholly hiss you." This is the lesson I learned from E.C. Cummings after studying his
poetry in one of my college English courses. But I learned it earlier, much earlier,
from my father.
At the pond, my first memory of us together, sitting at the banh of the water,
summer sun all around us, the ducks begged for bread at our feet. You can't feed
them all was the syntax of things. But feed them. The lesson my father taught me.
My tenth birthday and he was away at Busch Cardens on a Kadima trip. He missed
my party, but crept into my room past midnight, careful not to wake me. He'd
bought me a teddy bear, tucked it between my arms, as I pretended to sleep.
Feeling was first.
The trophy I wanted to bring home after my piano competition in 1986, the one
where I played Beethoven's Pathetique. I came home with tears instead and he
bought me my own trophy, called me a winner, anyway. Since feeling was first.
"What? You want to major in Creative Writing? What will you do with that? Ah—
okay, okay, just do it. Do something. Do what you love." Feeling was first.
At my son's bris, "May Cod bless him and keep him. May Cod shine his light upon
him." Here, the syntax mattered, I know. But feeling was still first.
Even last month, when I came home for Passover, my father gave me a copy of
one of his favorite books, "Don't Sweat the Small Stuff." Because feeling is first.
My father lives a life of significance because of his philosophy. While others are
scavenging for power and money and their personal positions in life, my father is
camping in the mountains, listening to the stars, and feeding souls with his wis¬
dom. My father has touched and influenced more people than he will ever know.
And in turn, they will touch and influence people. He is a modern-day, Jewish Thoreau.
In the end, this is all that matters really, ho one will remember what you said last
Tuesday at the meeting, if you bought your suit at Meiman Marcus, or if you use
Tide instead of the store brand. It's who you are, what you've done, and whom
you've touched with your presence that really matters. And this is what my father
taught me. The mind has a thousand eyes, but the heart just one.
for 20 years of devotion to the youth of our congregation

The City That Saved Itself
A city saved itself.
It was marked for destruction
because it was a city of violence.
Yet it was not destroyed
because its people turned from violence.
Nineveh was the city. And Jonah was the prophet
who proclaimed its destruction.
The prophet had to be taught
why the people were spared.
He did not understand at first
why the city was permitted to save itself.
And what Jonah learned about Nineveh
each of us must learn in our life.
Our errors need not be final
Inevitably, we make mistakes.
We cannot be perfect. We are human.
We imagine that the best way to protect ourselves
is to concentrate on ourselves.
We withhold. We pull away. We try to possess.
These are common mistakes.
And often we continue to make them
even though we know they are mistakes.
Yet we also have the power to defeat our mistakes.
We can transform error separating us from others
into a good which brings us together.
At any point in our lives we have the power
to regret a mistake, to learn from it.
Not merely to be sorry but to accept responsibility
for our errors and for the hurt we cause someone else.
We can atone.
And when we do, we can feel as our pain
the pain we have caused others.
We learn that to share pain as to share love
joins us with someone else.
We close the space between man and man.
Certainly, each of us can catalogue
a long list of errors we make.
And these will differ as we are different.
The ancient liturgy includes such a list:
the errors almost all of us must strive to revoke.
Let us consider one of these:
an error which has plagued many generations
and has become increasingly acute in our own time.
The sin we have committed
in lowering our parents and teachers
For many generations we have tended to think of
the Commandment to honor our father and mother
as it concerns only young people and their parents.
But no time limit was set on the Commandment.
And now that science has bestowed on so many
the gift of longevity
we are essentially unprepared for it.
We have to learn how to remain children to our parents
after we ourselves have become fathers and mothers.
We have yet to learn that
to keep the generations of a family together
everyone must be given the opportunity to serve.
To fulfill the deepest need of the aging
we must allow them to give of their wisdom;
to continue to serve, not merely to be served.
And what of our teachers?
Everyone knows we must have the teacher and school
to help us give wisdom and character to our children.
Yet in the midst of our abundance
we turn away from the teachers in whose hands
we have put the development of our children.
In the ways we fail to honor our parents and teachers
we reject the wisdom we need for ourselves.
As we benefit from the gifts of our time
we do not sufficiently remember
that progress consists of more than going forward.
It must also mean going inward.
And when we do this we will remember
that wisdom builds the house
and the teacher is the guardian of the city.
We will remember that each of our errors
properly understood and corrected can lead us
to still richer rewards of wisdom
of love given and love received.
“For the sin we have committed in lowering
our parents and teachers, and for all our
errors, O God who forgives...bear with us,
pardon us, grant us atonement. ”
— From the Prayers for The Day of Atonement
To all our friends, a year of peace and good health, and to children everywhere,
we pledge our efforts to improve the world.
The Jewish Theological Seminary of America
The Seminary is the academic and religious center of the worldwide movement of Conservative Judaism.
Believing that its message for the New Year 5760 is of compelling importance, we have arranged for its publication.
East Coast Fruit Co.
Mrs. Louis Portnoy and Families
with the hope that it will nurture some reflections on the High Holidays. It is presented with wishes for a healthy, happy New Year.

A Message from Rabbi Gaffney
Rosh Hashanah and the New Millenium
By my calculation, as I write this
message, there are 189 days left in the
20th century. Imagine, December 31,
1999. A day later we will be entering
the new millenium. One reads
everywhere of plans people are making
to observe this milestone. For example,
many have booked a cruise which will
take them just west of the international
dateline so that they will be the first
people to greet the 21st Century. Others
have purchased expensive tickets for a
concorde flight that will take them
around the world as they want to begin
the new century by experiencing it in
24 different time zones.
No doubt about it. We have
entered a special year which many
people have been anticipating for a long
time. I recall, as we began this decade
in 1990, telling a friend, a rather elderly
person, that in ten years we would be
marking the new millenium. He replied,
rather wistfully, that he was not sure that
he would be around to celebrate this
event. God willing, most of us will be
able to mark our years in two different
centuries. This is indeed a privilege. It
was Einstein who taught us that there is
a space - time dimension. Time is
relative. Sixty minutes on one’s watch
is psychologically longer to the person
waiting by the telephone for the results
of an important medical test and far less
to the person engrossed in relaxing or
vacationing in an idyllic setting. Time
is relative.
The Jewish Theological Seminary
was right on target when it described
how Jews should celebrate the
millenium. The Seminary characterizes
people who are taking cruises to exotic
places and scheduling airline travel to
mark the millenium as people who are
practicing alchemy of the imagination.
Millenium celebrants are turning an
ordinary sunrise, on January 1, 2000
into a moment electric with meaning.
I know ...I know ...you will say an
ordinary sunrise? The daybreak of a new
millenium? Well, yes! Unless you figure
that God is bound to the decimal system.
For God who is eternal no thousand-year
measure is intrinsically divine. No date
on the human calendar is.
Time isn’t handed to us with
significance built in. What we have
instead is a partnership. God gives us
time ...and it is we who give time its
meaning. Marking time would be
irrelevant were it not for the fact that
we shape its character. The eve of
Rabbi David and Inge Gaffney
January 1st of the year 2000, for
example, will occur on a Friday evening.
At the same time that many will flock
to exotic settings, many Jews will be
gathering, with their families, to
celebrate Shabbat with prayers, with
family singing in familiar surroundings
and with loved ones seated around the
festive table. And they will mark the
juncture between the old and the new in
this fashion. This ceremony will not be
a party in recognition of the New Year
...but rather a prayer meal ushering in
the Shabbat which begins at sundown
every Friday and ends after sundown on
Saturdays. We invest time with meaning
when we can say I love you after 20
years ...30 years or 50 years of marriage
despite the hardships and the
difficulties. Time is significant and holy
when a congregation can stand with
reverent silence which is pierced only
by the sound of the Shofar awakening
our conscience. The milestone whose
passages we mark with importance is the
day that we finally get the diploma ...or
that first moment we look into a crib
and see the miracle of new life. These
are moments holy in time.
The prayers we will be offering
on the High Holidays express this
notion. One important sentence found
in the Mahzor is...“vyayda kol pa-ul kee
ata p’alto” ... “when every creature will
know that You created it; when every
living thing will recognize that You
fashioned it”. The prayer states that we
should each look upon ourselves as
God’s creatures? Why? Abraham J.
Heschel, one of this century’s most
significant interpreters of Judaism once
pointed out that in the Jewish view, man
is not man because of what he has in
common with the earth but because of
what he has in common with God. The
Greek thinkers sought to understand
man as part of the universe; the prophets
sought to understand man as a partner
of God. To consider ourselves as God’s
partners is a challenge to continue to
create with God that creature God meant
us to be.
In one of William Stegner’s
novels there is a scene dealing with this
theme. It takes place in a small town in
Vermont where opportunities for young
people are limited. A teacher becomes
interested in a young boy who shows a
great deal of promise. She encourages
him to leave the little town and look
elsewhere for greater educational
opportunities.
“I’d take this chance to go to
college if I were you”, she tells him.
“There won’t be much else we can teach
you around here. You would stay the
same size all your life.”
The risk of staying the same size
all of our lives is as great in Jacksonville
as it is in a sleepy New England town.
And it is not only our minds that must
continue to grow to reach our full God-
given potential. We must mature
emotionally and spiritually as well.
Where our truly human dimensions are
concerned ...the dimension of the mind,
heart and soul -we must not stay the
same size all of our lives.
The year 5760 which will begin
this Rosh Hashanah may have
something more significant to teach us
than the year 2000. Rosh Hashanah and
Yom Kippur teach us that we can make
something of the millenium ...or of any
day if we choose to. That is what we are
here for, isn’t it? Not to wait in hope or
dread, at daybreak on some particular
day, for something or someone to come
from outside and change our lives. Rosh
Hashanah 5760 is here to teach us that
we have the God-given gift to infuse
every day with meaning because time is
ours to fashion and to shape. Rosh
Hashanah 5760 teaches us that we need
not be passive in the face of
uncontrollable forces but it is in our
power to contribute to making each
experience good.
As we begin a New Year and a new
millenium let us resolve to shape these
events, to recognize that we have been
given the gift of time ...and the gift of
life and it is God’s gift to us to infuse
each ordinary day with meaning
...whatever day it is.
As we mark the sacred milestone
of 5760, Inge and I wish all of our
members and friends a year made holy
by human intention. Shana Tovah! 5

Rabbi Kentof’s Message
The academic year 1999/2000
marks a milestone in the life of our
congregation as well as in our personal
relationship with the Center. For more
than twenty years, Alina and I have
labored with love to build up a
meaningful Education and Youth
Department. As a result, we have been
successful in raising a generation of
Jews who carry with them warm
memories of their involvement with our
formal and informal educational
programs, as well as a sense of pride
in their Jewishness.
In the fast-paced world we live in, there are many
cases where young people feel left out and left behind.
Too often these kids fall between the cracks. With so
many households dealing with both parents working
outside the home, the synagogue must step in to help
bring these children into the loving arms of Judaism where
they can find acceptance. This is, after all, the best place
(after home) to nurture growing children and help them
find themselves and their place in modem Jewish society
so they can lead a meaningful Jewish life.
The youth and education programs here at the
Center are designed to make a positive impact on the
lives of our students, giving them the tools they need to
make Jewish decisions, and in time be instrumental in
their adult decision making as well. Hopefully, they will
look back with fond memories at their time at the Center,
and remember what they have learned, which in turn will
help them choose the right path to follow.
Our schools include a Sunday
School for kindergarten through 2nd,
and an Afternoon Hebrew School for
grades 3 through 7. We also participate
in the Beit Midrash Community
Hebrew High School for students in
grades 8 through 12.
Our Youth Department offers
three excellent clubs that are affiliated
with United Synagogue Youth:
Chalutzim (grades 4 & 5), Kadima
(grades 6-8) and USY (grades 9-12).
Each group participates in a variety of
religious, cultural, social and athletic activities throughout
the year including camping, retreats, conventions, summer
youth programs and Israel pilgrimages. We take pride in
our programs which are often held up as the example by
which other synagogues’ programs are built. We hope
that you, as individuals and as a congregation, will take a
close look at our programs and make every effort to get
your children involved, and keep them involved, in as
many aspects of Center life as possible. Remember that
involved children grow into involved adults.
Alina and I wish you and your family a Happy &
Sweet New Year.
—Rabbi Dov Kentof
Rabbi Dov Kentof
Director of Education & Youth

Cantor Heller’s Message
As we approach Rosh Hashanah
for the year 5760, many of us, myself
included, look expectantly to the dawn
new millennium. Certainly we foresee
this being a once-in-a-lifetime experience
to bring in a new century and, with it, a
host of new hopes, prayers and
aspirations. Despite all this, we don’t
anticipate anything magical or super¬
spectacular is going to happen at
midnight December 31,1999. The most
amazing co-incidence, however, is that
it will occur on a Friday night, which
should remind us as Jews that the special
and significant occasions should be celebrated with family
and in the presence of God. What better forum can there
be other than around a Shabbat Table.
So, as we reach the final Rosh Hashanah and Yom
Kippur of this century, I wish to recall some of the
highlights of the past year, which I will use as a springboard
into the year 2000.
As a teacher, I am proud to have prepared some 29
of our students who affirmed their close ties to Jewish
values and tradition by celebrating their B’nai Mitzvah
on our pulpit this year. Their enthusiasm and diligence
bodes well for the future, as they continue on a path of
Jewish education to eventually take their places as our
leaders of tomorrow. The vibrancy and activity I
experienced in the hallways of the Beit Midrash, the
community Hebrew High School, is a joy to behold. I
was privileged to teach in this forum, comprised of
students from different levels of observance, who were
bound together in the common goal of discovering more
about being Jewish.
My first Cantor’s Concert was a resounding success,
including my choral group from the Solomon Schechter
Day School. Next year, a new and exciting musical
program is planned that will instill in them a love and
understanding of how the Jewish pulse beats in pace with
its music.
Challenges still remain. The
world is changing around us at
lightening speed, pulling us in many
directions. We need to find an anchor.
A factor that remains constant under
these diverse conditions, I believe, is
tradition. Maintaining our Jewish
tradition—laws and customs—and
pursuing its message through
education with continued energy and
commitment, from the youngest child
to the eldest adult, surely is the answer.
To paraphrase the Rosh Chodesh
meditation, life passes by us like a
fleeting shadow, each and every moment is precious to
be used to its fullest.
With this wonderful Center and its enormous
resources, I approach the New Year with a new resolve
to continue this process, and pray that our tefillot (prayers)
will be heard in the innermost chambers of the Heavens,
blessing each and everyone of us with a year of health
and happiness.
Shana Tova, from me and Lynette, and our children
Gary, Lisa, Jonathan and Daniel.
— Cantor Aron Heller
A final run through for Alex Whitman’s Bar Mitzvah.
Cantor Aron Heller
Cantor Heller performing with the Orange Park
Chorale.
Guest soloist Ralph Nussbaum with Cantor Heller rehearsing with Solomon Schechter
Lynette and Cantor Heller students for the Cantor’s Concert.

Executive Director’s Message
The countdown has begun. While
the new millennium is only months
away, the Jacksonville Jewish Center
is busy planning for its own milestone
— the Center’s 100th birthday — to
be celebrated in the Fall of 2001.
While not quite on the scale of
entering the new millennium, the
Jacksonville Jewish Center’s Centennial
carries its own significance. The story
of our synagogue over the past 100
years is truly remarkable. The Center’s
impact on Jewish life in Jacksonville
is immeasurable.
Over the next two years, we will
be paying tribute to the Center’s past,
while laying the foundation for its
future. Many exciting events are being
planned. How fortunate we are to have
the opportunity to celebrate such a
milestone.
Even as we prepare for our
Centennial, daily synagogue life
continues at a rapid pace, adding
important chapters to our synagogue’s
history. This past year’s calendar was
jammed packed with more than 40
lifecycle events, stimulating educational
programs, recreational activities, youth
conventions and social events—all
aimed at meeting the needs of our
diversified congregation.
Bruce Horovitz
Executive Director
Our Samuel and Esther Galinsky
Educational Programs continue to
thrive with the Solomon Schechter Day
School attaining a record enrollment.
Our Preschool and Hebrew Schools
provide the finest programs in their
areas.
This past year also featured
continued improvements to our
physical facility including providing all
new air conditioning units in our
classrooms, and the continued
installation of a new, highly efficient
energy management system to govern
our entire building. More improvements
are planned as we continue our
Centennial Challenge campaign to
refurbish our facility.
All of this activity would not be
possible without the day to day efforts
of our dedicated staff, committed lay
leaders and valued volunteers. It is the
combined efforts of these groups that
make the Center such a vibrant institution.
A special Mazel Tov to Rabbi Dov
Kentof for 20 years of devoted service
to the youth and families of our
congregation and the community.
As our own Centennial approaches,
we have much to be to be thankful for.
We have a rich tradition that has
positively impacted Jewish life for
more than five generations.
Our brightest promise, however,
is in our future. The Center stands
poised to enter the next hundred years
ready to meet the challenges that lie
ahead. With a clear vision and the
continued commitment of all of our
members, we will be up to that
challenge.
It is written: “The best way to
predict the future is to create it.” Let
us all work together to create the future
of the Jacksonville Jewish Center.
On behalf of Edith, Sam and Max,
I wish you a happy and healthy New Year.
— Bruce
Activities Director’s Message
There is a Talmudic passage that sums up the essence of why we do, what we do at the
Jacksonville Jewish Center — “as my parents planted for me and my generation, so must I
plant for the next generation.” As we begin preparations for the coming year, and plan for the
next century, we are planting for the future of the next generation, which is a sacred task
superseding everything else.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center is exemplified by a cadre of devoted men and women
who place the priority of planting for the next generation above all else. I am privileged to
work with so many selfless people who take on enormous challenges, and show such creativity,
enthusiasm and passion. They recognize the profound truth that although we Jews may call
ourselves am olam, an eternal people, our eternity is exceedingly precarious. It is the
responsibility of our generation to ensure our survival. Take the opportunity to become
involved. Let us know what you we can do for you and your family. Let us resolve to make it our duty: to plant for the future.
On behalf of my family, Abe, David, Talie & Dana, my best wishes for a year of health, happiness and peace. L’Shana Tova.
Shirley Bielski
Activities Director
— Shirley

President’s Message
ShanahTovah!
We are a special congregation with a
proud legacy.
Of the twenty-two members of our
talented confirmation class last year, five
were at least the third generation of their
families to be confirmed at the Center.
We have a number of families whose
membership at the Center span many
decades, including six generations of our
family who have been Center members.
As a third generation Center officer, I
view this history as more than just statistics. These are
important examples of the rich heritage we have built at the
Center — the foundation from which we assure the Jewish
future of our children and grandchildren.
We have been working hard to make that foundation
even stronger. The Education Committee, under Michael
DuBow’s leadership, has made great strides for our Galinsky
Educational Programs. Our Educational Endowment Funds
are at the highest level ever. For the first time, joint funding
from the Federation and the Center offers Solomon
Schechter Day School financial aid to members of other
synagogues.
Next year we will have a technology coordinator for
grades pre-K through middle school to provide state-of-the-
art computer instruction to be integrated with the general
and Judaic curriculum. The committee is also in the planning
stages of substantially upgrading the computer lab and
equipment.
The Finance Committee, led by Michael Shorstein, has
worked to meet the challenge of financing expanding
synagogue services and programs. The committee has been
faced with balancing additional program requests from
members, educational needs of our schools, and increasing
maintenance needs of our building with traditional funding
limitations.
All of the major committee chairmen, including
Philip Bloom (House), Rhoda Goldstein (Membership),
Sheila Jacobson (Building Fund), Kathy
Osterer (Youth), Harris Rittenberg and
Joanne Davis (Adult Education) Joe Shacter
(Religious), and Elyse Whitman (Cemetery)
have been investing tremendous talent and
energy to upgrade and enhance their
respective areas.
The officers and board are committed
to continuing the Center’s history of Jewish
excellence for you, our members. Among
the priorities of the coming year are:
• We will begin planning for our 2001
Centennial celebration, which will consist
of a series of special events and dedications.
• We will begin the next stage of the strategic planning
process including obtaining member feedback, setting
committee priorities, and establishing a mission statement.
Our goal is to ensure that we understand and are meeting
our members’ needs and expectations.
• We will reinvigorate our membership efforts and ensure
we’re effectively communicating all that we have to offer.
• We will continue efforts in the community on behalf
of our schools to ensure availability to any Jewish child
desirous of a Jewish Education.
• We will complete the payoff of the building mortgage,
and direct efforts to upgrade our facility to first class
condition.
In partnership with you, our members, the board of
directors looks forward to an exciting year. On behalf of the
officers, executive committee and board, and all who
volunteer and work to make our Center exceed your
expectations, we wish you a very good year.
Mazel tov to Peggy Organes, Janis Dorman, and all the
yearbook staff and volunteers for another excellent yearbook.
Beth, Daniel, Rebecca, Jennifer, Benjamin and I wish
you a healthy, happy and prosperous new year!
— Mark J. Shorstein
Mark J. Shorstein
President
Berman Bros., Inc.

EducationalEndowments
“V’Shenantam Lvanecha”— “Teach it to your children”
— Shema
Knowledge has been the means by which Jewish life has flourished for thousands of
years. The Jacksonville Jewish Center is extremely pleased to announce the establishment of
three generous endowments that will enable us to enhance and perpetuate Jewish education
for generations to come.
The Samuel and Esther Galinsky Educational Programs
From the estate of Esther Galinsky, an extraordinary endowment has been established as a perpetual
memorial, in keeping with Mrs. Galinsky’s request that the gift be used to enhance the Jewish education
of children attending our schools. The Samuel and Esther Galinsky Educational Programs, comprising
of the Solomon Schechter Day School, Center Pre-School and Religious Schools, will now be the
recipients of long needed educational enhancements. This gift will benefit not only the more than 400
children enrolled in our schools today, but students well into the future
Esther and Samuel Galinsky immigrated from Russia during the first decade of the 20th Century,
met and married in this country. Mrs. Galinsky, who died in 1997 at the age of 104, was a sweet,
unpretentious and kind woman, and a longtime member of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. She took a
keen interest in the Center’s educational programs and delighted in visits with the students.The generosity
of Mrs. Galinsky’s bequest will allow the Jacksonville Jewish Center to provide a Jewish education of
the highest quality.
Alix Schaul Levy Scholarship Fund
The Alix Schaul Levy Scholarship Fund scholarship fund has been endowed by Albert Levy in
memory of his beloved wife, Alix, who was a devoted advocate for the children of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center. Among her many activities, Alix served as president of the Solomon Schechter PTA and
played a major role as a leader of our Girl Scout Program. The endowment will provide tuition scholarships
for those needy and deserving students wishing to attend the Solomon Schechter Day School. Mr.
Levy’s generous contribution will significantly expand the outreach that we have to our community and
to our children.
Sol and Hilda Goldman Scholarship Fund
The Sol and Hilda Goldman Scholarship Fund is a permanent endowment to benefit worthy and
needy applicants desiring admission to the Solomon Schechter Day School. Sol and Hilda Goldman
have been lifelong members and dedicated supporters of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Sol was honored
as the Center’s Hatan Torah at Simchat Torah services this past year. This gift will help afford a Jewish
education for all of our children.
On behalf of all of our members, and most especially of our children,
the Officers and Board of Directors of the Jacksonville Jewish Center
expresses its deepest appreciation and gratitude for these special endowments.

===== PAGE 55 =====
Organizations
Center Men’s Club
The Jacksonville Jewish Center Men’s Club is a committed organization dedicated
to the enrichment of Center life and the Jacksonville Jewish Community. The Men’s
Club provides an opportunity for our Center men to work together supporting, sponsoring
and participating in programs that bring meaning to our youth, companionship to our
elders, and involvement in our Jacksonville Jewish community.
Our Men’s Club is also an active member of the Federation of Jewish Mens Clubs,
an international organization dedicated to the ideals, philosophy and traditions of
Conservative Judaism. Through the support of the Federation we distribute hundreds of
Yellow Holocaust Memorial candles every Yom Hashoah to our Congregation families.
This year we will be continuing the successful “Hearing Men’s Voices” program; a series of workshops dedicated to creating
a forum for discussion and sharing issues relating to being a Jewish man in today’s world. These workshops will cover family,
religion, society and health in thought provoking and fulfilling agendas.
We are committed to our Center Youth with programs like the Yad Shel Chai (Hand of Life) award that rewards our
youth for reading Torah, The Youth Achievement Award for outstanding Confirmation students and presentation of a book to
every Bar and Bat Mitzvah. We also work closely with Rabbi Dov Kentof supporting the Center youth organizations by
helping out with conventions and Religious Schools programs.
We bring companionship to the elders of our community with programs like the River Garden Fishing Day which
brings buhbes, zaydes and kids of all ages together to try their hand at emptying the River Garden pond.
We support the Jacksonville Jewish Federation and the Jacksonville Family and Community Services with tzedakah
programs and are involved with the community at large by volunteering on Christmas to allow our friends of other faiths to
celebrate their holiday with their families.
We are visible friends of our Center as well, with programs like selling and building Sukkot for
our Center families and helping to decorate the Center’s Goldman Sukkah. You can find Men’s Club
members at the Lag B’Omer Picnic every year selling hot dogs and cokes. We form a corps of ushers
for services every Shabbat and holiday. Men’s Club members sponsor, lead and participate in Men’s
Club Shabbat and Armed Forces Shabbat, and take an
active role in preparing B’nai Mitzvah students of
all ages by sponsoring events and providing tutors
and assistance.
These events have earned your Men’s Club
First Place and Honorable Mention Torch Awards
from the Federation of Jewish Men’s
Clubs in years past, and we are actively
pursuing new Torch Awards for
community service and other events.
The Men’s Club is also a great
way for friends to have fun. Every
summer we unite for the infamous
deep sea fishing trip and take our
families with us to a Lizard Kings
hockey game and a Jacksonville Suns
baseball game. In the Fall we “kick off’
our new year with a membership drive party featuring games of chance and
raffles while we watch a Jacksonville Jaguars away game on a BIG screen TV.
All this and more is waiting you if you are at least 21, male and a member
of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Join now, we need your fellowship!
— Dave

Beth Shorstein
President
It is with great pleasure and anticipation that I begin
my second term as president of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center Sisterhood. This past year has flown by, filled with a
multitude of exciting and fulfilling programs and activities.
Our educational programs have been rich and satisfying.
We continue to offer the monthly Sisterhood Seminars, ably
led by Rabbi Gaffney, and were delighted to celebrate with
the successful students of the Sisterhood Adult B’nai Mitzvah
class. Women’s Minyan this year had a wonderful new focus,
incorporating elements of a healing service and explanations
of the daily amidah. Sisterhood Shabbat was spiritually
uplifting and satisfying, and demonstrated the many talents
of our diverse membership. Book reviews, social action and Israeli affairs reports
continue to keep our members informed and involved. Last, but not least, our
bereavement committee continues to bring comfort and aid to Center families in
mourning.
This year our monthly meeting programs were varied, as well as entertaining. We
educated our members with '‘Wheel of Fortune” at the opening meeting, informed them
of community needs with our visitors from P.A.C.E., learned about hat making and table
decorating at the Paid-Up Membership meeting, to name a few. The annual joint
Sisterhood/Men’s Club meeting incorporated a shalosh seudot, which was enjoyed by
all. Finally, our closing meeting, which honored eight very deserving, long-standing
Sisterhood members, was a perfect ending to a fabulous year.
Thanks to this year’s successful Torah Fund campaign, a generous donation was
made to the Jewish Theological Seminary. Our delightful luncheon appropriately honored
Blanche Slott, who was instrumental in making Torah Fund an important Sisterhood
project.
Sisterhood involvement with the youth of our congregation included providing
snacks for the children at high holidays, baking hamantaschen for the students, and
running the arts and crafts booth at the Joint Sisterhood Purim carnival. Leagrams to
our Consecration and Confirmation students provided additional funding for all of the Religious Schools and youth programs
at the Center. We thank you for your generosity! Also, we have begun to work closely with the PTAs of all three schools,
and are busily planning new joint programs for the coming year. Stay posted — wonderful changes are in store!
Be sure to support Sisterhood, with your membership and by getting involved with one of our many fundraising
activities. Watch the Bulletin and Chai Lights for news of our annual New Year’s flower and cards, sales, as well as of some
new and wonderful events.
Our Center Sisterhood is very fortunate to have an
involved, interested, and active board, filled with new and
returning members of all ages. We are proud of our many
activities, and welcome new ideas and volunteers. Bring
your energy and enthusiasm to our opening meetings, and
find out what we are all about!
Mark, Daniel, Rebecca, Jennifer, and Benjamin join
me in wishing you a Shana Tova, and the hope for a peaceful
and happy New Year.
— Beth
Left: Etta Drashin, Bonnie Sandler, Maxine Romo, Beth Shorstein, Barbara
Safer and Marsha Pollock attended the Florida Branch Spring Convention,
May 1999, in Tampa.

Organizations
Jewish Singles
Center Choral Society
Jacksonville offers fun in the sun and jobs to many
Jewish singles who have recently located here. It also offers
social opportunities to any Jewish single age 21 to 40ish.
Group get- togethers are a great way to meet people, make
friends and do important networking. Often the group meets
to have a kosher dinner, usually in someone’s home. The
activities are varied and interesting.
Singles receive three-month calendars letting them
know what is going to be happening, when and where.
This program continues to be sponsored and funded by
the Jacksonville Jewish Center and the Jacksonville Jewish
Federation, which allows dinners to be very affordable.
For additional information, call Rhoda at the Center
office, 292-1000.
55+ Club
Row 1 L to R: Molly Silverman, Pollie Copans, Sada Freedman,
Row 2 L to R: Jack Silverman, Philip Copans, Ben Edwards
The 55+ Club, now in its twelveth year, usually meets
the second Sunday of each month from 2 to 4 p.m. at the Center.
Programs include speakers, musical performers, dancing
exhibitions and interesting presentations. The entertainment is
always exciting, refreshments are served and there’s always
time for socializing. The annual luncheon in June is the
highlight of the year. Membership is open to the entire Jewish
Community with annual dues only $12.50 per person.
For over 52 years, the Center Choir has been enhancing
services on the High Holidays and Shavuot with moving
liturgical music. Over the years they have given numerous
concerts featuring members of the choir as well as nationally
acclaimed soloists. Many of the current members have been
singing with this group for twenty years or more. New
members are always welcome.

Committees
Mitzvah Committee
Joan Waitz, Chairperson
Bikkur Holim literally means “visiting the sick” and is
a religious imperative for us as Jews. The ancient tradition
finds its roots in a number of biblical and rabbinic texts.
In Bereshit, the first book of the Torah, we learn that
human beings are created b’tzelem Elohim, in God’s image.
Judaism understands this to mean that we are required to
emulate the ethical attributes of God, mercy, kindness,
forgiveness, graciousness.
Modern society with its wondrous advances in medical
technology, intimidating procedures and sterile hospital
environments, has, if anything, increased the need for Bikkur
Holim. The needs of the sick and aged require, not only the
best of scientific knowledge, but the loving care of family,
friends, neighbors and the synagogue community.
“To minister to the sick, is to minister to God.”
— Abraham Joshua Heschel
For more information and to volunteer your time, call
Joan Waitz, 731-2486.
Daily Minyan Captains
Traditionally, public worship requires a minyan, a
quorum of ten. Only in the presence of a minyan may the
Torah be read or the Kaddish prayer be recited.
Our dedicated minyan captains ensure that there is a
minyan twice every single day of the year. It is often a hard
and thankless job. It requires hours of phoning to coordinate
attendance.
It is because of them that you can recite kaddish when
you mourn or observe yarhzeit. We owe the captains not
only our thanks, but also our participation, in order to
continue providing a minyan for each service. Please call
any minyan captain and become a “regular” for at least one
minyan a week.

Membership
Rhoda Goldstein, Chairperson
Rhoda Goldstein and Judy Greenfield
prepare welcome bags.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center was so named because
those who were involved recognized that we had to be more
than just a place of worship to those who affiliated.
As we approach a new millinium, we realize that need
more than ever before. With so many families living far from
their parents and their children, we understand the importance
of giving those who relocate to Jacksonville a support
system — a place for people to make friends and find an
extended family.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center provides a place for
education, socialization and recreation, as well as a place
for meditation, stimulation and contemplation. We attract
new members, and retain those who have already joined us,
by providing for their needs.
The Membership Committee, along with many of the
Center’s other committees, plays a vital role in planning
opportunities for our members. The committee is always
looking for dedicated people to participate, because there is
always so much to do. You can help us accomplish our goals
by becoming a member of the membership committee, by
letting us know when new people have moved to Jacksonville,
by welcoming newcomers when you see them during
services or an activity, or by extending hospitality during
holidays or on Shabbat.
Special brunches encourage prospective members to join the Center.

Henry Kramer Library and Resource Center
Minnie Schreiber and May Goldie Stillman, Chairs
The Tofield Reading Room of the Henry Kramer Library is very attractive
and inviting. Current magazines, such as Moment, and newspapers, such as the
Forward, are available for the membership to peruse and enjoy.
Many new books have been added to the rich collection of books of Jewish
interest —the Bible, Talmud, prayers, the Holocaust, Israel, philosophy,
biographies, poetry, humor and fiction.
The Steinsaltz Talmud in Hebrew and English is available, and reference
materials are accessible for study assignments.
The Library is open on Sundays 8:30 to Noon.
Shabbat Torah Readers
Ted Gross, Coordinator
There were two interesting
features of the new Torah Readers in
the past year. One was the increasingly
large percentage of girls who have
opted to read more than one Torah
portion for their own, and for their
friends’ Bar and Bat Mitzvahs. Second
was the length of the portions they have
selected, some choosing to read all the
portions available in a Sedra. This is in
keeing with the general trend in gender-
related opportunities for women. We
are proud of the advancement, and hope
it will bring more satisfaction to the
administrators of our educational
programs, and to the members of our
congregation.
Those who joined our team on a
continuing basis were: Josh Berkman,
Sasha Bloom, Robyn Blum, Dena
Mizrahi, Lindsay Morris, Whitney
Rosenberg, Laurie Sandler, Debbie
Shacter, Sammy Warfield and Alex
Whitman.

Committees
High Holiday Preparation
It takes many volunteers, many, many hours, to prepare a synagogue for the
High Holiday season. We at the Jacksonville Jewish Center are indeed fortunate
to be able to count on our dedicated volunteers and are very grateful for all the
help they give Richard Ford and his maintenance staff.
The 1998 Selihot program planned by Robin Morris and Shirley Bielski,
“Shabbat - A Day For the Rest Of Your Life”, launched a year of programs centered
around the celebration of Shabbat in the home and synagogue. Heartwarming
personal experiences and memories by Polina Brunfon, Moti Demri, Ellen
Goldstein, Rabbi Dov Kentof, Faye Maiman, Shira Morris, Seth Moser,
Natalie Osterer, Craig & Sherry Sommers and Dot Yerstandig inspired the
congregation to reacquaint themselves with their own family traditions.
Preparing talitot for the congregation to wear during Rosh Hashanah and
Yom Kippur is an enormous labor of love. Thank you to Ava Axelrod and Shirley
Bielski for washing all the talesim and to Beverly Geller for unpacking the new
talesim. Ava Axelrod also baked delicious honey cakes for the Religious Schools to
sell.
Gayle Bailys, Justin Axelrod, Shirley Bielski, Svetlana Goldiner, David
Schmerer, Jason Schmerer, Steve Warfield and the JJC maintenance staff
rearranged and inserted the new plaques in the Memorial Wall. William Rose
checked machzorim for Chalutzim and Kadima to place, along with additional
pamphlets and handouts, on the seats of the expanded sanctuary. As in the past,
USY polished the silver and cleaned ritual objects.
House Committee
Last Rosh Hashanah, the
leadership of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center introduced the “Centennial
Challenge” - a five year program that
would allow us to go forward with
improvements and repairs necessary to
maintain our commitment to enter the
next century in a debt-free, refurbished
building.
Because of the generous support
of many of our congregants, the
following improvements have been
completed and more are under way:
additional bathrooms in the school wing,
repairs to the skylights and roof, handicap
access to the bima and new carpeting in
the sanctuary, refurbishment of the
kitchen, including new equipment and
an institutional refrigerator,
resurfacing of cracked decking in the
pool area, new carpeting in the
Preschool, and installation of a
security system for the building.

Committees
Adult Education
The Jewish people have historically been called the
“people of the book” We have a tradition of love and
dedication to education, and Jewish education in particular,
as a lifelong pursuit of the highest esteem. To answer the
diverse educational needs of our congregation, the Adult
Education Committee, with the guidance of Rabbi David
Gaffney, coordinated an ambitious program with a wide
variety of courses and speakers throughout the year. Hebrew
literacy, Talmud, synagogue and holiday workshops, Firing
Line and other discussion groups formed the basis of a
comprehensive, ongoing curriculum. In addition, Rabbi
Arnold Stiebel conducted several Shabbat Learner’s Minyans and Etta Drashin introduced “Read Hebrew
America”, a crash course sponsored by the National Jewish Outreach Program The Setzer-Levit Memorial
Lecture, Shabbaton and community-based engagements brought prominent speakers of national and international
acclaim. Chairman Harris Rittenberg and the entire committee invite you to avail yourself in the coming year
of the exciting programs that are now being planned to enrich your spiritual life.
The Adult Education Committee continues to enlighted us with
nationally acclaimed speakers like Professor Shalom Paul.
Rabbi Irving “Yitz” Greenberg was the featured speaker at the Shabbaton
Weekend, underwritten by the Marilyn and Louis Safer Shabbaton Fund. Rabbi
Greenberg, a Harvard doctoral scholar, founder of the National Jewish Center for
Learning and Leadership, and champion of pluralism within Judaism and the greater
world, revealed his vision of Judaism’s continuing triumphs and challenges in the context
of 21 st century secularism, freedom and affluence. His warm, affable style and delightful
humor inspired as well as educated the congregation throughout
the weekend.
Rabbi Irving Greenberg
Abraham H. Foxman, Director of the Anti-Defamation
League of the B’nai Brith, captivated a packed audience on a Tuesday evening as the
guest of the Setzer-Levit Memorial Lecture series. Foxman is regarded as one of the
great leaders in the American Jewish community and is world-renowned for his fight
against anti-semitism, bigotry and discrimination. A Holocaust survivor himself, Foxman
is at the forefront of issues such as Christian-Jewish and Black-Jewish relations, as
well as Holocaust restitution. It was an electrifying program.
Two opportunities to meet with representatives of the Jewish Theological Seminary
were afforded our congregation. Dr. Jack Wertheimer, the Mendelson Professor of American Jewish History
and the Director of the Ratner Center for the Study of Conservative Judaism at the Seminary, spoke at the home
of Laurie and Linda Dubow. At Seminary Shabbat, rabbinical student Rick Chisever, lead services and spoke
enthusiastically about life at the Seminary and his hopes for the future as a Conservative rabbi.
Family University, directed by Pam Maman, offered Solomon Schechter Day School students and their
parents an opportunity to study together from one-day curriculum of varied topics. “Shabbat — A Day for the
Rest of Your Life”, coordinated by Harriet Brodsky and Robin Morris provided a year of do-it-yourself
projects to encourage families raise their level of Shabbat observance.

Special Events
Simhat Torah Honorees
The High Holiday season reaches its joyous climax on Simhat Torah, the last day of the festival
of Sukkot. Because of our perennial love of the Torah, it has been referred to as Israel’s “bride”. On
Simhat Torah, we bring to a close the annual cycle of the reading of the Torah and immediately
begin anew. An ancient tradition evolved of honoring worthy men by calling them to the Torah for
the last reading, Hatan Torah, and the beginning portion, Hatan Bereshit. In our synagogue tradition,
we also honor the contributions made by a “Woman of Valor”.
Sol Goldman
Hatan Torah
Sol was born in
Jacksonville, the
son of Russian
immigrants who
worked hard to
support their family
and insisted he
receive a Jewish
education. Sol has
always been a member of the Center. He
celebrated his Bar Mitzvah at the 3rd &
Silver St. shul.
Sol’s father was a kosher butcher
and taught him the meat packing business
from childhood. After graduating from
Robert E. Lee High School, Sol and his
bother Joe started a small slaughtering
house that gradually grew into the Gold
Merit Packing Company. He married his
wife Hilda in February 1958.
Sol is someone you can count on
at the Jacksonville Jewish Center. He
attends Sunday minyan regularly and is
always available to be the 10th man when
called at the last minute. On Shabbat
mornings, you can always find Sol in his
“regular” seat with his warm smile and
friendly handshake.
Over the years, Sol has given
generously of his time and resources to
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Along
with the Goldman family, Sol and Hilda
helped dedicate the beautiful Goldman
Sukkah shortly after the Center relocated
to its current facility.
Most recently, the Sol and Hilda
Goldman Scholarship Fund was
established as a permanent endowment to
benefit those needy and worthy students
desiring admission to the Solomon
Schechter Day School. “We feel the
greatest contribution to Judaism is
through the education of the children and
we are thankful we can help those in
need,” Sol said.
We were pleased to show our
appreciation to Sol with this honor.
Evan Yegelwel
Hatan Bereshit
Evan, our immediate
past President and
current Chairman
of the Board, has
helped guide the
Jacksonville Jewish
Center through some
of its most exciting
and challenging
times. Under his stewardship, the Center
re-enforced its financial foundation and
developed a leadership team to meet the
demands of the next century. He did it
all with a sense of history, humility and
humor.
A native of New Jersey, Evan
graduated from Rutgers College in 1977
and from the University of Florida,
Holland Law Center in 1980. Upon his
arrival in Jacksonville, Evan immediately
immersed himself in the affairs of his
community and his synagogue. He has
served for more than 10 years on the
Board of the Jacksonville Jewish
Federation and was the first president of
the River Garden Hebrew Home for the
Aged Foundation. He serves on the
national executive committee of AIPAC,
the Florida Regional Board of the
AntiDefamation League and most
recently was appointed to the Board of
Directors of the United Synagogue of
Conservative Judaism, Southeast Region.
Nowhere, however, has his
commitment been more steadfast than at
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. He has
given tirelessly of his time, energy and
resources. Evan is not only active in the
administrative affairs of the synagogue,
but also serves as a Sunday minyan
captain.
Evan and his wife Arlene were
honored with the “Independence Issue
Award” at a recent Israel Bonds Dinner.
Together with their daughters Esther and
Tania, we wish them all a well deserved
mazel tov.
Kathy Osterer
Woman of Valor
Kathy exemplifies
the true spirit of a
“Woman of Valor”.
A wife, mother,
daughter, sister,
granddaughter and
friend to many, she
gives to everyone
and everything with
energy, enthusiasm and commitment.
Originally from New York, Kathy
Osterer became involved in her
synagogue and her community, almost
from the day she arrived in Jacksonville.
A past president of Women’s American
Ort and the Center Sisterhood, Kathy has
held numerous chairmanships and board
positions with Hadassah, Center
Sisterhood, River Garden, Jacksonville
Jewish Federation and her daughters’
schools. Kathy has served as Vice
President of Membership for the
Jacksonville Jewish Center and currently
serves as its Youth Vice President. A
Kadima advisor for the past five years,
Kathy is actively involved in planning
conventions, housing hundreds of
teenagers every year and supervising
monthly youth meetings.
A fundraiser extraordinaire, Kathy
chaired the 1995 River Garden Foundation
Gala and co-chaired the very successful
1998 major Center fund raiser, “Raffle
for Education.” Her special love, the
Sisterhood Judaica shop, has thrived
under her chairmanship.
Currently Kathy is employed by
River Garden Foundation as its Director
of Development
Honored for her devotion to
family, friends, and synagogue, Kathy
shared this simcha wth her beloved
husband, Morrie, four daughters Natalie,
Jessica, Melissa and Ashley, her father,
Jerry Fixel, as well as her entire family.
May she and her family be blessed with
many years of health and happiness.

Special Events
Adult B’nai Mitzvah
June 18-19, 1999 — 26 Tammuz
My B’nai Mitzvah Class Experience
By Karen Dubin
Almost THREE years of study ... it seemed like
an eternity, now it seems like only yesterday when we
began our class with Rabbi Gaffney. Every Wednesday
evening at 7:30, we met with Wendy Brezin for Hebrew
and with Rabbi for our class. We studied Jewish history,
life cycle events, holidays and so much more! Rabbi
showed us everything, from how to properly put on a
talit, to how to light Shabbat Candles.
Our discussions covered many topics and our
opinions were very diverse. We were the B’nai Mitzvah
Class of “Why?” — we had MANY questions! There
was so much to learn, so much to understand. And, it
was fascinating. Rabbi Gaffney made the class very
interesting.
The Hebrew at times seemed impossible.Wendy was demanding, but she made the class fun and the reading
became easier with practice. Lois Gosa and I wondered many times if we could possibly chant the Haftorah. Even
though we volunteered to do it, we were very uncertain. Thanks to hard work, Wendy’s and Peggy Organes’ help we
accomplished it. The sense of achievement echoed through the class .... Jeff’s Torah introduction, Gary and Michael’s
Torah portions, Ava’s Kiddush, Cindy’s Yigdal, Marcia’s explanation of the Haftorah, Lauren’s Mizmor L’David
and Norman’s presentation of the class gift. Svetlana’s Uvenucho Yomar and Yakov’s “True and Certain” were
especially moving, since they were not only B’nai Mitzvah class members, but new Americans as well. Everyone
had a part and did it well.
In the beginning we were strangers; we became twelve good friends and committed Jews. It’s our beginning,
not an end. We have the rest of our lives to continue learning and sharing our love of Torah with each other, our
Synagogue and our families.
Thank you Rabbi Gaffney, Wendy Brezin, all the tutors, Center Sisterhood and everyone else who helped to
make this life cycle event possible for us.
The B’nai Mitzvah Class, R to L - Front: Lauren Hochrad, Ava Fixel,
Svetlana Goldiner, Lois Gosa, Rabbi David Gaffney, Karen Dubin, Cindy
Silverberg, Marcia Kleiman. Back: Yakov Goldiner, Michael Fixel, Gary
Kresel, Jeff Cramer, Norman Kleiman.
Yad Shel Chai Award
Yad Shel Chai Recipients.L to R Front: Jonathan
Tannen, Lisa Rand, Rachel Gerber, Jonathan Heller.
Back: David Organes, Mark Frisch, Jared Fleet, Sam
Horovitz. Not shown: Seth Finck.
Among it’s many activities, the Jacksonville Jewish Center
Men’s Club promotes positive youth programs. Two years ago, the
Men’s Club instituted the very successful Yad Shel Chai—Hand of
Life—award program which encourages and rewards post Bar/Bat
Mitzvahs who continue reading Torah on Shabbat, holidays, and at
minyan. Teens who read a minimum of 18 (chai) portions by age 18
are awarded a beautiful, inscribed Torah pointer (yad). To date, the
following youths have received their yads: Seth Finck, Jared Fleet,
Mark Frisch, Rachel Gerber, Jonathan Heller, Sam Horovitz, David
Organes, Lisa Rand, and Jonathan Tannen. We should all be extremely
proud of their accomplishments and hope to see many more join them
in achieving this high honor. If you would like more information
regarding this program, please contact Randall Smith.

Annual Center Raffle for Education
ShAltE, RaFFLe & Roll!
The Center’s Annual Raffle to Benefit Education was again a huge success this year. With thousands of
dollars raised, our children will reap the benefits of our contributions for years to come!
Perhaps the best part of the evening came when the Grand Prize of $10,000 was awarded to our own Solomon
Schechter Day School by an Anonymous Patron! We wish to sincerely thank those responsible for entering the
school in the raffle.
In addition, David Robbins won the Multiple Ticket prize of a same-day trip for two to a 1999 Jaguars Away-
Game, as guests of Linda and Laurie DuBow. Mary and Martin Wolf won the Early Bird drawing for a luxurious
beach weekend at Sawgrass, courtesy of Far East Brokers and Consultants, Inc. Congratulations to all the other
winners of the fabulous prizes.
As with all major events such as this, there are many people involved in all of the different aspects of planning,
and I wish to thank them so much for their tireless work and unparalleled devotion to the education of our children,
and to the growth of our synagogue’s schools. The raffle committee, ticket sellers, Center staff, and prize donors all
deserve our sincerest gratitude and appreciation for their hard work. Thanks, also, to all of you that purchased
tickets for the evening, and especially to our Grand Patrons and Patrons, and to our major sponsor First Union.
—Lawrence Ansbacher

Bernie Dean
Sunday, November 1, 1998
Direct from Broadway, the Tony Award
winning Jewish Musical, “The Rothschilds,”
was presented in a unique one-man
performance by singer/actor exciting
Bernie Dean. Working from a script
written by the creators of “Fiddler on the
Roof,” Dean tailored this rags-to-riches
saga to the small stage, performing the
musical score and portraying all the main
characters. He was humorous, tender and
thoroughly entertaining.
Catskill Frolics
Sunday, February 7, 1999
The hilarious Vic Arnell and Dick Lord, and talented singer, Holly
Lipton, brought a true slice of Jewish Americana to the Center with a
genuine Catskills-resort stage show. The audience laughed uncontrollably
at the comic antics and savvy political humor of the two zany comedians
and were moved to sentimental tears by the lovely songstress.
Ira Heller
Sunday, April 18, 1999
Singer Ira Heller performed an original
blend of traditional and contemporary
Jewish music A charismatic presence on
stage, Ira electrified the audience with his
lyrics and melodies that combine eternal
Jewish themes with issues of importance
in the modem world. His vocal ability and
boundless energy made this a thrilling
evening.

There once was a mensch named Dov Kentof,
who came here from points north and south.
He’s taught our children with stories
that have come from his head to his mouth.
There surely is someone in heaven,
who looked down on Jax from above,
and knew that we needed a person
whose name was Dov.
With Hebrew and history and Torah
he taught us by showing the way,
to do mitzvot and care for each other
and to do something good each day.
He came to us as Noah and Moses,
carrying his rod and his staff.
After goats and donkeys and chickens,
we’re waiting for a visit from a giraffe!
He set examples for Jewish living,
whether in campsites or in his back yard.
To observe Shabbat and kashruth is easy,
and he makes us think really hard.
LTI and Kadima conventions
were always the highlight of our year.
Even if our homes were overflowing,
our children would stand up and cheer.
Our lives you have influenced so often,
our children have gained oh so much.
How can we all ever thank you,
for each one of us you’ve managed to touch.
From your friends at
Dixie Sales Company

Special Events
“Touched By Dov”
Youth Banquet Honored Rabbi Dov Kentof
Sunday, May 16, 1999
Rabbi Dov Kentof has a magical way with kids.
Like the Pied Piper, he draws them to himself and to
Judaism. Like the angel on the well-known television
show, he touches their lives in so many wonderful ways.
Dov has a sixth sense for knowing what each child needs
to feel special and included.
In acknowledgment of the unique, 20-year
relationship he has had with our congregation, Rabbi
Kentof was honored at the Jacksonville Jewish Center
Youth Banquet on Sunday, May 16. Over 450 attended
the lovely dinner which featured a heartfelt tribute of
speeches, songs and presentations, made by
representatives of the Center’s Youth groups, adult and
family groups and his colleagues. Once again, all who
were there were “Touched by Dov.”
From left: Rabbi and Alina Kentof pose with their son, Gil, daughter,
Tali and her husband, Rob Whitely, and son, Zach.
Above: Robin Morris, Edith Horovitz, Isabel Balotin,
Marilyn Garber and Shirley Bielski serenade Dov.

Rabbi Kentof

to honor Rabbi Kentof.

Special Events
Shabbat: A Day for the Rest of Your Life
A United Synagogue-Rabbinical Assembly National Campaign
Shabbat has always been a cornerstone in Jewish life. If we get more members to engage
in exploring Shabbat, it will enhance their lives, give them a sense of Shabbat Shalom—
worry-free time, quality time for friends and family. Congregants will come to experience
personally the poetry of being Jewish. Many of our members have not experienced Shabbat.
Our challenge is to encourage our members to explore the richness of Shabbat at home and in
the synagogue—to take first steps and next steps. We are creating programs and environments
which will empower members to discover the joy and enrichment that Shabbat can bring,
providing knowledge, skills and encouragement as they “explore Shabbat.”
Seth Moser and Robin Morris share
Shabbat memories at Selichot.
An Overview of the 1998-99 Shabbat Activities
• September: Selichot Program to introduce Shabbat—A Day for the Rest of Your Life with
speakers of all ages and backgrounds describing their personal experiences and memories of
Shabbat.
• October: Bags containing Shabbat candles and the blessings were distributed to every student
in every school in the building every week in October. Also a sticker was included so that after
Shabbat the child could decorate a 3-D candlestick to show
how many people actually lit their Shabbat candles.
Sisterhood, Men’s Club, USY, and other groups received
bags of Shabbat candles, blessings and stickers at their
meetings during the month.
• November: Grape Juice bottles were packaged in boxes with the kiddush and motzi included.
One week, these were also distributed to schools and at activities throughout the synagogue.
• December: A special Board of Directors Havdalah was shared by many families. A complete
kit for Havdalah including braided candle, spices, wine and blessings was given to each family
to take home. These families were encouraged to invite other
families to join in their own Havdalah the following weeks.
Zach Morris, Max Horovitz and
Lindsay Morris prepare bags of
Shabbat candles.
Solomon Schechter Day School
Students assemble “kiddush” boxes.
• January: Day School Kindergarten Shabbat Dinner was a big undertaking. The students made
their own candlesticks, head coverings, kiddush cups, challah, and challah knives and covers.
Multi-generational families participated in this memorable evening. Consecration of the first
grades of the Day School and the Religious Schools had the
theme of “Shabbat.” The children sang familiar and not-so
familiar z’mirot, recited Shabbat prayers and famous
quotations about Shabbat, and even welcomed the Shabbat
Bride.
• February: Many members opened their homes and invited guests for Shabbat Dinner during
this month. Some invited people they knew and some were matched up with people they
didn’t know. Some did it one Friday night, and some did it several times during the month.
The response was terrific! Some comments:
“We had a delightful Shabbat with a very nice new family that we would have not met otherwise.”
“A great program to encourage Shabbat participation.”
Preschoolers demonstrate the rituals
at Bubbee & Zaydee Shabbat.
“We enjoyed our guests and are always happy to have Shabbos company even after February.”
• April: Family University of the Solomon Schechter Day School was held. The main theme of classes offered to parents and students
was Shabbat. Some of the classes were: Kabbalistic Secrets of Shabbat, Inner Meaning of Shabbat Rituals, How to make Havdalah,
Cooking for Shabbat, Blessings for the Family for Shabbat, Shabbat Story Telling, and Roadmap for Shabbat Morning Service.
Hopefully, this is just a starting point for Shabbat involvement for our membership. We all can experience
Shabbat to a fuller degree. Come to Tot Shabbat or Pre-Jr. Congregation or Jr. Congregation or Ramah Shabbat or
a Shabbaton. Shabbat happens every week, so take advantage of what it has to offer . . . and make Shabbat a Day
for the Rest of your Life.
— Harriet Brodsky and Robin Morris, co-chairs

Lifecycle Events
Burial and Mourning
Center Cemetery
The Center owns and maintains two cemeteries adjacent to
the Evergreen Cemetery in North Jacksonville. Both have beautiful
shade trees and mature landscaping. During the past year, a state of
the art sprinkler system was installed in the New Cemetery. The
new system includes water faucets for your use at each gate and at
the little house. In addition, Eli
Fleet has built beautiful
containers for stones for both
cemeteries as his Eagle Scout
project. This wonderful mitzvah
enables visitors to follow the
tradition of placing stones at
the gravesites of their loved
ones.
Both cemeteries are open daylight hours every day except Shabbat
and Jewish holidays. To reach the Old Cemetery, travel north on North
Main Street to Winona. Make a right on Winona and go until you reach
Gate 5. The Old Cemetery is on the left. To reach the New Cemetery,
travel north on North Main Street to 43rd Street. Make a right on 43rd
Street and the entrance is at the next right, which is Liberty Street.
Future plans include improvements to the entrances, as well as
additional landscaping. Contributions can be made to the Cemetery Fund
in honor or in memory of loved ones. All donations will be used to enhance
and maintain our cemeteries.
We thank the voulnteers
who serve on this
important committee:
Elyse Whitman, chairperson
Barbara Cantor
Charlotte Fialkow
Stacey Goldring
Dr. Jeffrey Goldstein
Stuart Hecht
Fred Kupperman
Leonardo Maiman
Morrie Osterer
Marsha Pollock

Eli Fleet with his Eagle Scout mitzvah project.
Hevra Kaddisha
Officers 1999-2000
President
Nathan C. Sandler
VP/Treasurer
Robert Bossen
VP/Education
Ira Grann
VP/Committee Chair (Tahara)
Ilse Gottlieb
Sally Becker
Sheree Spriggs
VP/Secretary
Naomie Bossen
During the course of a lifetime, no one can avoid an encounter with
death. It is an experience for which no one is ever really prepared. Judaism
is a faith that embraces all of life, and death as a part of it.
The care of the deceased immediately following death is the
reponsibility of the Hevra Kadisha., a group of dedicated Jewish laypeople
from our own community. They are knowlegeable in the traditional duties
and devoted to showing the utmost respect for the deceased. In addition to
performing the Taharah (purification) rituals, cleansing and preparing the
body for burial, the Hevra Kadisha recites the required prayers. In addition,
they provide a Shomer (watcher) to be at the side of the deceased until
burial.
Membership in the Hevra Kadisha has always been considered a great
communal honor. We in Jacksonville are very fortunate to have a group of
dedicated and sincere men and women, who perform this mitzvah for all,
regardless of financial means. They are available 24 hours a day throughout
the year to meet the needs of the Jacksonville Jewish community.
Anyone interested in joining may contact any of our officers.
—Nathan C. Sandler, president

— The Samuel & Esther Galinsky Educational Programs —
Early Childhood
Message from the Director
The Early Childhood Department is located in the Selevan Pre-School
Dale Schemer
Director
The Early Childhood Department of the Jacksonville Jewish Center
is entering its 48th year of providing quality programs to Jewish children
in the community, regardless of affiliation. Our ever expanding program
offers not only a Florida Kindergarten Council accredited preschool, but
also includes an extended day program available to all children enrolled in
the Preschool, Solomon Schechter Day School and Religious Schools.
The early morning and afternoon daycare, coordinated by veteran
preschool teacher, Cheryl Gartner, provides a healthy happy environment
in which parents feel secure in the knowledge that their children are well
cared for. Ballet, computer skills and soccer are just a few of the “Giant
Steps” programs offered during extended daycare. These outstanding
programs, plus a low staff-to-student ratio, are just a few reasons that the
extended day program continues to grow.
The staff of the Early Childhood Department recognizes the great importance of
the role we play in your child’s life. Each of our programs is planned to nurture the
social, emotional, and intellectual development of every child.
This past year has been rewarding, thrilling, and a growing experience for all
involved. We anticipate an even larger enrollment of enthusiastic and loving children
for the 1999-2000 school year.
— Dale Schemer
Shirley Thacker
Secretary

- 1999-2000 Officers -i

Marla Lewis

Helen DuBow

Allison Bailys

Meryl Rittenberg
Pre-School Liaison
Debbie Banks
The Solomon Schechter Day School PTA is a group of parents, teachers, administrators,
and students working together for a single purpose, the enrichment of our schools.
Both the Preschool and Day School benefit from the PTA on a weekly basis. Our
volunteers are truly dedicated to the children in providing quality programs throughout the
school year.
Through fundraising projects like the annual magazine sale, book fair, candy sales, and
the PTA Cookbook, we provide much needed equipment. Science program kits, playground
equipment, stencil machine equipment, computer software, and much more have been
purchased because of your participation in these projects.
Educational and fun programs enhance our children’s school days:
• Book-A-Bration honors children, teachers, and friends by directly adding to our library.
• The Cultural Arts Committee provides art enrichment programs. Volunteers serving
as the “Picture Lady” visit classrooms on a regular basis.
• Holiday observances such as model seders, Consecration, and Shabbat and Havdalah
boxes require the helping hands of the PTA.
• Red Ribbon Week for drug awareness, as well as vision screening, are needed health
programs that the PTA provides.
Your commitment to the PTA makes these programs and events successful. PTA provides a fun, often social, opportunity
for involvement in the education of our children. Try it out—pick a project —you may benefit as much as the schools!!
L’ShanaTova,
—Marla
1998-99 Solomon Schechter Day School Teachers and Staff

===== PAGE 113 =====
— The Samuel & Esther Galinsky Educational Programs
Solomon Schechter Day School
Willard M. Kennedy
Principal
At this time, we all turn our attention to a new year of our
children’s educational development. We know that, next to the
home, school exerts the most influence on the course of an
individual’s life, and that the relationship between home and
school is an extremely important one.
With a young child about to start formal education, we
look for school readiness; however, this step is just the first in a
process of transition that lasts right through the elementary years.
If we accept this idea of transition, it becomes clear that family
and faculty must adapt to each other so that students get the
most from their educational experience.
What are the signs of a nurturing academic environment
that encourages smooth transitions? In an issue of Kappan. a criterion was published to measure this.
These were:
1. Children like school — they like other children, teachers, classrooms, etc.
2. Steady growth in academic and social skills.
3. Parents are positively involved in their children’s education. (Since we are a religious institution,
supportive learning is especially important.)
4. Classroom environments are emotionally positive for teachers and students.
5. Home and school value each other. In a successful school, teachers
know the parents and value their input. Conversely, parents trust
and value the teachers, knowing they have the best interests of the
students at heart.
Solomon Schechter parents are exceptionally involved in the academic
lives of their children. This commitment, along with the fact the our school
provides a conducive learning environment where a child feels safe, secure
and comfortable enough to take risks in the learning process, earns our
school a top rating.
As your thoughts turn to 1999-2000, you can rest assured that your
choice of the Solomon Schechter Day School for your child’s learning
environment is the right choice.
Evelyn Borstein
School Secretary
— Willard Kennedy

Youth Congregations
Sunday School
The SUNDAY SCHOOL of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center is planned expressly for children in kindergarten, first
and second grades. A weekly, two-hour program gives each
child the opportunity to experience the richness of his/her
Jewish heritage through song, dance, role playing, cooking,
short stories, games, arts and crafts. Emphasis is placed on
the holidays and festivals. Jewish values and mitzvot are
encouraged. Many thanks to our wonderful Sunday School
teachers Susan Kadish, Sharona Kadish and Susan
Neadle.
Students included Daniel Abramson, Sarah Abramson,
Lillian Anderson, Cory Brenenson, Alyssa DeCrispino, Sarah
Kirkwood, Madison Kress, Danielle Lewis, Sarah Neadle,
